```
William M. Klein wrote:
> Arnold,
>    Did you actually use a "0" subscript?  It seemed to me in the old OS/VS 
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

Yes, Sir.  I actually used a zero subscript, although as a variable. 
I don't believe I used a literal zero subscript, but I would be happy 
to try it just to see if it works.

And I didn't do this with OS/VS COBOL.  It was at least IBM COBOL II.

I tried a test with Realia COBOL 1990, and it fails with a literal 
zero as subscript, but works with a variable subscript containing 
zero.  Using a literal -1 for a subscript won't even compile, but 
using -1 as the contents of a variable subscript works as expected, as 
  if there were two table entries preceding the first entry.

Here's my test code:
       *$CALL,COMP-5,DIALECT-REALIA4,LP:56
        IDENTIFICATION DIVISION.
        PROGRAM-ID.         ZEROSUB.
       ****************************************************************
       *AUTHOR.             ARNOLD J. TREMBLEY.                       *
       *DATE-WRITTEN.       08/12/2008.                               *
       ****************************************************************
        ENVIRONMENT DIVISION.
        CONFIGURATION SECTION.
        SOURCE-COMPUTER.        IBM-PC.
        OBJECT-COMPUTER.        IBM-PC.
        INPUT-OUTPUT SECTION.
        FILE-CONTROL.
        DATA DIVISION.
        WORKING-STORAGE SECTION.
        01  800-SUB                         PIC S9(4)   COMP VALUE +0.
        01  900-TABLE.
            05  900-ENTRY-NEG-1             PIC X(12)   VALUE 'MINUS'.
            05  900-ENTRY-ZERO              PIC X(12)   VALUE 'ZERO'.
            05  900-ENTRY               OCCURS 9 TIMES
                                            PIC X(12)   VALUE SPACES.
        PROCEDURE DIVISION.
        0000-MAINLINE.
            CALL 'DOSMODE'.
            DISPLAY 'ZEROSUB  08/12/2008'.
       *
            MOVE ZERO  TO 800-SUB
            MOVE 'ZERO' TO 900-ENTRY(800-SUB).
            MOVE 'ONE' TO 900-ENTRY(800-SUB + 1).
            DISPLAY 'VARIABLE SUBSCRIPT'.
            DISPLAY '900-ENTRY(-1)= ' 900-ENTRY(800-SUB - 1).
            DISPLAY '900-ENTRY(0) = ' 900-ENTRY(800-SUB).
            DISPLAY '900-ENTRY(1) = ' 900-ENTRY(800-SUB + 1).
       *
            MOVE 'ONE' TO 900-ENTRY(1).
            DISPLAY 'LITERAL SUBSCRIPT'.
       *****DISPLAY '900-ENTRY(-1)= ' 900-ENTRY(-1).
            DISPLAY '900-ENTRY(0) = ' 900-ENTRY(0).
            DISPLAY '900-ENTRY(1) = ' 900-ENTRY(1).
       *
            GOBACK.

and here are the test results:

ZEROSUB  08/12/2008
VARIABLE SUBSCRIPT
900-ENTRY(-1)= MINUS
900-ENTRY(0) = ZERO
900-ENTRY(1) = ONE
LITERAL SUBSCRIPT
900-ENTRY(0) = ONE
900-ENTRY(1) = ONE

Perhaps I should try this on Enterprise COBOL for z/OS, just to be sure.
```

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>

```


"tlmfru" <lacey@mts.net> wrote in message 
news:el%nk.9116$i15.476@newsfe01.iad...
>
> Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
…[84 more quoted lines elided]…
> frustrated rage.

Ah, thank you Peter. So, THAT'S why I piss people off without meaning to... 
:-)

Seriously, I think it is important to have confidence and certainty, once 
you have figured things out and arrived at a conclusion, but I certainly 
wouldn't advocate closing the mind to new evidence or being arrogant about 
being right.

Last night I was in a pub quiz with a group of friends (6 of us). For some 
reason (I didn't volunteer...) they decided I would be the team leader and I 
made certain that everyone was happy with our answers before we handed in 
our slip for each round. There were two "incidents" that were very apropos 
what you describe above:

1. Which country does the America's Cup team "Allingi" represent? I just 
wrote "Switzerland" in the answer and one of the girls said: "I don't think 
so... are you sure?" I said "Yes, but we can talk about it if you want..." 
She went all petulant and said" "Oh well, If you're SURE, but you better 
well be right, Mister." I smiled and said:" OK"  When it was found to be 
right, I never said a word. She just looked at me, but never said anything. 
It was my certainty that upset her. We are actually good friends and I 
wasn't phased by it.

2. Name one of the ingredients of a "Singapore Sling"? I remembered from 
time spent working two jobs when I first got into computing 40 years ago (my 
evening job was as a cocktail barman at one of Auckland's leading tourist 
hotels...) that Singapore Sling was Gin based (being of a data processing 
bent, I had started building a "database" of cocktails, with the base drink 
for each of them representing the root of the hierarchy, so I knew what was 
Gin based, Whiskey based, Brandy based... etc.) I was certain that the 
Singapore Sling was Gin based, but I couldn't remember the other 
ingredients. One of the other girls, who had spent some time working on 
cruise ships said: "It's Cherry Brandy!!!" but she didn't know any of the 
other ingredients.  So here we have a dilemma: I am certain of Gin, she is 
certain of Cherry Brandy, and both of us are doubtful about the other one's 
answer... :-)  I find it very hard to compromise with my own certainty (I 
think most people do), but age and experience have taught me to try and keep 
the bigger picture in mind and not lose perspective... It is a Pub Quiz, no 
lives are at stake...let her have Cherry Brandy... I was just about to write 
it, when she said: "Oh, hang on, I might be confusing it with something 
else..." They were waiting for our slip so I wrote "Gin" and handed it in. 
Gin was right. So was Cherry Brandy. She said to me: "You snapped at me when 
I said 'Cherry Brandy'..." I didn't think I did, but I apologised anyway 
because it certainly wasn't my intention. She loosened up after finding that 
we had won 12 shots of Jagermeister (her favourite) for that round, by 
getting the most right answers out of the 7 teams.

We won the contest by 1.5 points and got a bar tab of $100, which we cleaned 
up easily (with food) so we had a good night out, a lot of fun, and it 
didn't cost us anything. But, on reflection, I think your point about people 
being bothered by certainty is very true.

The question is whether we SHOULD be bothered by it. Is it better to THINK 
you had breakfast this morning or to KNOW you had breakfast this morning? No 
problem. But when we get to metaphysics and "certainties" become 
diametrically opposed (I don't believe in God, the Easter Bunny, or Father 
Christmas, though I respect these ideas and would not try and disillusion 
anyone adhering to them, but, If I meet someone who is equally certain that 
any or all of them are real...), then I guess sparks are likely to fly. I've 
had some fabulous conversations around this with one or two 6 year olds. 
They are not in the least bit embarrassed by their certainty. (I have taken 
great care not to shake it in any way...the world will do that soon enough.)

I think the unacceptable side of certainty is when it becomes arrogance. 
This implies "I KNOW this but you are much too stupid to..." Or, when the 
certainty means the mind is absolutely closed and no further discussion is 
possible, or new evidence can be presented.

In both these cases, I would agree that another's certainty is simply 
annoying. In all other cases, I see nothing wrong with being certain about 
something, PROVIDED you don't need to enforce it on others, and provided you 
can have others not agreeing.

For myself, I'd rather argue with someone who makes a strongly stated case 
than with a wishy-washy "slippery eel" who is more interested in having an 
argument, than in actually establishing the correctness or otherwise fo 
stated positions.


>
>> >His programs probably work and are fairly eay to
…[31 more quoted lines elided]…
> freezeup if words like "now" are used in his presence!

:-) I remember many years ago being dragged along to a Dianetics lecture by 
some Scientologist friends. I thought it was "interesting" but I never 
signed up for courses. (I remember making some comments about one of the 
"bulletins" mimeographed in red from Saint LRH, where the phrase I used was 
"hysterical rubbish"... a very large lady, dressed in ominous black and 
reminiscent of a land-based Orca, called The Guardian quickly escorted me 
from the room and I wasn't invited back. They had correctly pegged me as a 
"trouble maker"... More power to them. After watching Tom Cruise I don't 
think I missed much. I have friends to this day who are practising 
Scientologists (I've asked them how long they have to practise before they 
achieve perfection...) and we get on fine. Sometimes we even discuss 
philosophy and the Human Condition. We are still friends.

Pete.
```

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()

```
In article <6gd4nhFeuvc0U1@mid.individual.net>,
Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

[snip]

>But, on reflection, I think your point about people 
>being bothered by certainty is very true.
>
>The question is whether we SHOULD be bothered by it.

Leaving aside the jocular 'are you sure about that?' it might be of 
benefit to consider 'that which is' to be of paramount importance when 
dealing with others and keeping 'that which SHOULD be' (caps original) as 
a personal standard-to-be-achieved.

>Is it better to THINK 
>you had breakfast this morning or to KNOW you had breakfast this morning?

I would say the answer to such a question depends on many variables.  
Decades on back the mother of a woman I knew was showing signs of senility 
and was placed in a housing-for-the-elderly project... the terms I am 
using are so out-of-date that it may be apparent how long ago this 
actually was.  Both the mother and the daughter were Jews who had escaped 
from Germany shortly before the Holocaust.

One day while talking on the telephone the mother mentioned, quite 
casually, that Yom Kippur had come rather early that year.

(explanatory note: the Jewish calendar (based on the Babylonian) is lunar 
and does not coincide with a solar calendar; Yom Kippur is a solemn 
holiday on which one atones for ones sins, fasts and prays for 
forgiveness and charity. a ram's horn is blown, as well... I'm sure there 
are Very Good Reasons for all of this)

The daughter, knowing that it was not Yom Kippur, gently asked the mother 
why she believed this... and that's when a sweet sadness came forth.

The elderly woman was hungry, she had not eaten.  Nobody is certain, of 
course, exactly what went on in her unravelling mind... she may have 
thought, for whatever reason, that the day was, in fact, Yom Kippur and as 
a result did not eat...

... or she may have forgotten to eat... and later in the day, noticed her 
hunger... and knew that the only reason she would feel that hungry at that 
time was because it was Yom Kippur, when she was fasting... so she knew it 
was Yom Kippur.

Given a choice between that kind of certainty and doubt... I'm not sure, 
of course, but I believe I'll choose doubt.

DD
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Any better ideas?

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2008-08-10T16:48:33+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<AZEnk.256410$bC5.231619@fe07.news.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<6g20pvFdnc3oU1@mid.individual.net> <7ed83766-31f3-4700-862d-9d069fac9591@m73g2000hsh.googlegroups.com>`

```
Concerning,
  "All COBOL code should be written to be totally independent of a  particular 
compiler or OS."

I can (sort-of) understand constraining your code to ANSI/ISO '85 conforming 
source code.  However, Tony's requirement for being '74 conforming for "new" 
code is simply silly (IMHO).  (Notice his use of all CAPS and no END-IF's).

There are places that I know of that still have a '74 Standard compiler 
available (and that are doing new development and maintenance in COBOL. 
However, I don't (personally) know any of such sites that do not ALSO have an 
'85 conforming compiler.  I *might* use old techniques if maintaining a program 
written to the older compiler's standard, but I certainly would NOT do new 
development that way.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Any better ideas?

- **From:** Robert <no@e.mail>
- **Date:** 2008-08-10T11:57:24-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4q6u941vq5lhal4bc1l7jqm550cdeor0ik@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<6g20pvFdnc3oU1@mid.individual.net> <7ed83766-31f3-4700-862d-9d069fac9591@m73g2000hsh.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Sun, 10 Aug 2008 04:48:39 -0700 (PDT), foodman <foodman123@aol.com> wrote:


>
>      MOVE SPACE TO W-TARGET.
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>

THIS CODE WOULD NEVER PASS PROD REVIEW. IT HAS LITERALS. YOU'RE NOT ALLOWED TO USE
LITERALS. ALSO, IT DOESN'T HANDLE EXCEPTIONS. HERE'S THE RIGHT WAY:

12400-DATE-PARAGRAPH.
    MOVE C-9000                   TO WS-TARGET.
    IF  WS-LENGTH = C-9001
         STRING W-DAY W-MONTH W-YEAR2
             DELIMITED BY SIZE
         INTO                                 WS-TARGET.
    IF  WS-LENGTH = C-9002
         STRING W-DAY W-MONTH W-YEAR
             DELIMITED BY SIZE
         INTO                                 WS-TARGET.
   IF  WS-LENGTH = C-9003
        MOVE W-SOURCE  TO       WS-TARGET.
   IF WS-LENGTH NOT = C-9001 AND C-9002 AND C-9003
       DISPLAY 'SOMETHING IS WRONG'
       DIVIDE C-0000 INTO WS-LENGTH.
12400-DATE-PARAGRAPH-EXIT.
      EXIT.
```

```
I worked on one whose data names were made of unprintable characters. A line of code
looked like:

     =  ** *SQRT(     ) + COS(  )

On the core program of a major credit card company, the Honeywell Cobol compiler said it
was out of memory, so they put all literals in two arrays -- one alpha and one numeric.
Imagine 100,000 lines of code like this::

IF (CUSTOMER-TYPE = LITA (36) OR LITA (54) ) AND
    (CUSTOMER-STATUS NOT = LITA (102) AND LITA (37))
    ADD LITN (204) TO SERVICE-CHARGE.
```

```
On Tue, 12 Aug 2008 10:11:21 -0500, "tlmfru" <lacey@mts.net> wrote:

>
>
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>code is going to generate a lot more object code than using literals would.

The symbol table was full. Literal subscripts don't generate much excess code because they
are resolved to an address at compilation time. Data names as subscripts would have
generated substantial excess code. 

A compilation took one hour, which was scheduled between production jobs. There wasn't
time for a second compilation. In case of error, the compiler generated code with a zero
operand, which I had to patch.




>
>PL
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

- **From:** Ron <NoSpam@Spamstopper.net>

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

```
"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in
news:6g20pvFdnc3oU1@mid.individual.net: 

> I have a situation in COBOL where I need to take a string and pull 
> characters out of it.
…[39 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

Getting into this late. Down computer. I admit I haven't gone through 
this whole thread but it appears no one has mentioned the feature 
designed to do this - UNSTRING. 

01  target-8
    	05 ddmm-8.
    	    	10 dd-8 pic xx.
    	    	10 mm-8 pic xx.
    	05 yyyy-8    pic x(4). 
    	05 redefines yyyy-8. 
    	    	10 cc-8 pic xx.
    	    	10 yy-8 pic xx. 


UNSTRING SOURCE DELIMITED BY '/' OR SPACE 
INTO DD-8 MM-8 YYYY-8. 
MOVE TARGET-8 TO TARGET-6.
MOVE YY-8 TO YY.
```

```
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> "data police".  Information security may or may not be a part of IT (I

Insider access and manipulation are also issues, as well as data integrity.
Generating an Access Audit record/row/segment is often a business 
requirement, even if no data is added, revised, or deleted.

I installed Data-Xpert and IMS-Xpert at a site back in the 1980s (now
merged with the competing FileAid product line).

About 10 years later I got a phone call from someone who identified himself
as a senior Internal Auditor asking what FileAid for IMS was. Thinking for a
couple of seconds before answering I said that FileAid for IMS/DB2/MVS were
products that let non-technical users diddle data without regard for business
rules. That seemed to be the answer he was looking for.

A check into monthly txn counts showed the application he was concerned about
having thousands of accesses a month. To me that was either a missing 
application function or someone routinely manipulating data outside the
business rules.
```

- **Message-ID:** `<RwOok.28112$IK1.6574@news-server.bigpond.net.au>`

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> I was told that RM COBOL cannot randomly access an ISAM file using a 
> secondary key, with READ... KEY IS.
> 
> Instead, it is necessary to use a START on the secondary key, then issue a 
> READ NEXT.

I worked on this product many years ago and I ssure you that this is not 
correct. I agree with the other posters that the issue probably 
originated as a statement about how to process duplicate keys ... in 
which case it has nothing to do with RM-Cobol, it's just a statement 
about duplicate keys ... which of course can only arise on an alternate key.
```

<a id="t-d8f9047430"></a>
## Thread: Join on mulltirow queries — 3 msgs

### Join on mulltirow queries

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-08-14T22:44:33+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6gigkkFg23tbU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
I did say I'd try expanding the 63 host variables in a base table, by 12 to 
match the 12 balances in an attached table to see if the single join would 
work. (every single host variable, along with it's attached indicator 
variable, would have to have OCCURS 12 added to it...)

I have to say, I started to do it and it just looked so horrible, I 
couldn't.

Im going to settle for a base access followed by single multirow access to 
any attached tables. If there is much difference in performance, I don't 
believe it will be significant. (If I'm wrong about this and performance 
looks like being a problem, THEN I'll HAVE to revisit it.)

Another possibility would be to use ADO and get a resultset right off the 
bat, but I simply don't have time to go this route now. Fujitsu COBOL 
requires ESQL with ODBC so that's what I'll use.

Sorry to disappoint anyone who was following this... :-)

Pete.
```

```
On Thu, 14 Aug 2008 22:44:33 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"

>I did say I'd try expanding the 63 host variables in a base table, by 12 to 
>match the 12 balances in an attached table to see if the single join would 
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
>Pete.

Pete,

From what you have told me about your customer, performance wont be an
issue.

But for your own info, dong single row accesses as you mention can be
the difference between running a job in 10 min or running same job in
30 hours. And this is by own experience of programs converted from
COBOL Indexed files to SQL without due consideration to performance,
and that I had to change.


Regarding your host variables, and indicator variables. indicator are
probably not required if you can live with the small performance hit
of using coalesce to return a non null value instead. 64 host
variables aint much. In the present client I am working with, one of
the tables has over 400 columns at the moment. COBOL I/O modules do
have all those referenced and no issues with it.

I can also supply you a few ADO samples, not the crap ones fujitsu
supplies.

Regards


Frederico Fonseca
ema il: frederico_fonseca at syssoft-int.com
```

```
No. These are random interactive accesses (the application is written mainly 
in PowerCOBOL). It will access a single row of the base table, then do a 
multirow access to any attached tables. Obviously, a single join of the base 
and attached tables would be better, but it simply doesn't work. For 
multirow access it needs a Cartesian join, so the Base would need to load 
the same row 12 times, while the attached table loaded its 12 balances. I 
just couldn't bring myself to code it... it was really butt ugly... :-)

BTW, I haven't forgotten about sending you the final code. I am still 
working on it, but you will get it when I'm happy with it. (Like, a final 
draft... I'll incorporate any suggestions you care to make)

liked those clumsy indicator variables...) Thanks.



>64 host
> variables aint much. In the present client I am working with, one of
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> supplies.

I have actually used ADO with Fujitsu some years back, just to try it out. I 
think I am too far down the path to go back now, but I'll keep your offer in 
mind.

BTW, the recent big Security Update release from MicroSoft caused all my 
Fujitsu COBOL components that were using ODBC to fall over :-)

It was a major trauma. Anything using ODBC (odbc.info control file) simply 
abended and called F3BDKSNP.exe, which then threw its hands up in horror and 
proceeded to loop indefinitely.... Not much help from a program supposed to 
handle abends :-)  I was able to attach to it through VS 2008 and get a 
disassembly and trace that showed it was trying to emit a message with a 
code in it. According to Fujitsu docs, "contact tech support with this 
code"... yeah, right :-)

After nearly three days of tracing and tracking DLLs, I found what was 
causing the problem and fixed it. (I won't go into it all here, but I can 
say it wasn't the fault of the MS updates; they simply triggered something 
that was wrong anyway.)

Thanks for your support, Frederico.

Pete.
```

## Thread: Re How competent are we — 35 msgs

### Re How competent are we

- **From:** "seasidesam" <sam@seaside>
- **Date:** 2008-08-17T13:45:20-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<28aad$48a8708c$62148bf3$10578@ALLTEL.NET>`

```
i am a member of the data processing dinosaur club; i learned the art of computer programming
in 1969 on a computer system used to kill people and to keep people from killing me, using
the most fundimental programming tools (buttons on the front of the box) and communicating
with the computer using it's own language... machine language.

i was taught cobol in an environment that i believed was common for the early '70s.  i was
instructed in the skills required to turn a senior analysts design into an executable that would
produce the results specified in the design.  technical knowledge of the computer system
was not required as an application programmer.  and i was allowed two years to become
proficient with the language.

i have been listening to (and reading of) arguments for the elimination of cobol as a data
processing tool;  most of the time in amusement, some times in disgust.  i learn more of the
declining ability of people to communicate than i learn of the 'problems' and the 'solutions'.

management has been a problem from the time the position was created... in any department
or profession.  management will be a problem as there are managers.

whenever you put those with one set of qualifications on a project requiring a different set
of qualifications the product will be something less than desired.  again.  in any department
or profession.  from my experience as a junior programmer, as a p/a, in tech support, and yes;
as a project manager and dp manager, the area of qualifications (training) has and is the
root of all dp problems (problems that i/we have control of).  and my experience was that
in this area the greatest returns on investment were achieved.

there is nothing 'bad' about cobol.  it is a tool.  it is more than adequate in doing what it was
designed to do.  it and when it goes away it will do so because of 'political' considerations.
this is an open forum, but plese do not send me a twenty-page single-spaced list of things
that are wrong with cobol.  any serious discussion on this subject would best be served if
held at your (or mine) favorite watering hole (with you buying)... and i drink a high-dollar
single-malt scotch.  but i would much rather discuss...

1) the absence of sex in my life (leads to a more-enjoyable life)
2) free-form cobol (tremendous gains in productivity in this area)
3) skillset certification (a hard one to implement but would do wonders for the profession)
4) single-malt scotch

cobol has issues.  identify.  rectify.

___________________

it is hard to understand how some of the junk software on the market today is...
marketable.  on those rare occasions that i dig into how the program is put together i am
left with a bad taste in my mouth at the sorry state of programming represented by
these programs.  i finally came to understand the situation.  what i see is a fresh graduate
of the cracker jack school of computer hacking, hired at a reduced rate by
a company driven by bottom-line rather that product quality, expected to produce a business
system using breaking-and-entering skills and tools.  i am slowly learning to live in a world
of reduced expectations.
____________________

as always...
nice day here.  hope yours is the same.

sss



--------------=  Posted using GrabIt  =----------------
------=  Binary Usenet downloading made easy =---------
-=  Get GrabIt for free from http://www.shemes.com/  =-
```

```
"Binyamin Dissen" <postingid@dissensoftware.com> wrote in message

I agree wholeheartedly, Binyamin.

In fact, there was a time (on a machine with very limited memory and 
resources in general) when we found it indispensable.

And we didn't end up with a tangle of spaghetti, either. We simply devised a 
few simple rules and stuck to them.

But, reading this thread of late, I find it quite saddening.

It seems like a bunch of grumpy old men grizzling about how awful their 
management are and how their fantastic technical talent is/was so superior 
to the youth of today but they never got to express it because they were 
suppressed by short sighted administrators, blah, blah yada, yada....

Some samples taken from the thread:

"i have been listening to (and reading of) arguments for the elimination of 
cobol as a data
processing tool;  most of the time in amusement, some times in disgust.  i 
learn more of the
declining ability of people to communicate than i learn of the 'problems' 
and the 'solutions'.

management has been a problem from the time the position was created... in 
any department
or profession.  management will be a problem as there are managers."

So, "management" are responsible for the decline of COBOL?

It had nothing to do with the rejection of OO COBOL by COBOL PROGRAMMERS? 
(NOT managers...managers generally don't know enough about technology to be 
able to accept or reject it...they use what they are told to use.) Had the 
COBOL programming community embraced OO COBOL when it first came out, the 
fate of COBOL may have been significantly different from what it is proving 
to be... but, it is hypothetical, we'll never know...Certainly the OO 
paradigm has been embraced by rest of the world and it is hard to imagine 
serious computing in today's world without it.

The fact that the whole world has moved on and found better solutions more 
appropriate to today's architectures is not considered. The fact that the 
traditional rigid hierarchic structure of management is being subsumed in 
more progressive companies by a networked management structure is also 
conveniently overlooked. Just a total introspective focus on how good it was 
and "poor me"...

... Things are changing. I don't like change. There's no need for it to 
change; I can handle it fine the way it is. Now I'm all bewildered by the 
change. That's what you get when you let managers run things...

Sad.

Another one...

"My theory has always been that popular software is written by really
brilliant people fresh out of community college who, having no experience of
the real world, have no idea how a "good" program should act and how it
should be written. "

So you can't write good software unless you have years of experience?

Maybe... I would agree that experience certainly helps and most of us try to 
make each program we write better than the last one we wrote (I do that 
still today), but is it really fair to dismiss the youngsters coming out of 
colleges today, having paid their dues, spent years of study and having had 
to grapple with stuff that we never did, as if the only area they can be 
faulted in is their lack of experience?

I have worked with several teams comprised of people like this and I have 
been very impressed. These kids are smart, keen, and their attitude is 
right. They are good at what they do (at least, the ones I've managed 
were...). They are eager to learn and they are not living in "Glory Days". 
Contrast that with some of the posts we are seeing here. And they have 
experienced people placed with them so that both can benefit. It certainly 
did me good to work with these teams.

Yes, there is good and bad software. Sometimes the assessment of it is 
purely subjective and a package or program that one person finds abysmal, 
someone else finds fantastic.

Yes, there are good and bad programmers. Always have been, always will be 
(at least, as long as people are required to do programming...) It is the 
diverse nature of Human Beings, not any particular suppression by 
management, or lack of experience, or departure from the accepted norms of 
1959 that causes the variation in software quality.

And yes, there are good and bad managers.

None of the above has anything significant to do with the decline of COBOL.

A stone adze was a reasonable tool for making a dugout canoe. A power tool 
is better. That doesn't invalidate the stone adze.

(Of course, you might decide that, having a power tool, perhaps a dugout 
canoe might not be the best way to cross the water, and so build something 
completely different... Then you'll have wailing and gnashing of teeth at 
the replacement of the dugout by the clinker built dinghy... it is the 
nature of things.)

What the Hell is this exclusive club of "oldtimers" who were the only people 
who ever wrote "good" software, who did it using good solid procedural COBOL 
constructs (the ONLY way to program computers) , did it in spite of 
horrendous know-nothing managers, and had the rug pulled out from under 
their feet by a world that just doesn't get it...?

Take a look at yourselves.

It isn't pretty.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Re How competent are we

```
being fun, I'd quit.

When it comes down to sad old men reliving Glory Days and blaming the rest 
of the world for their current situation, I see no place for me in it.

> [snip]
>
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> accomplishment of a stated Executive goal'...

(Hmmm.... smells like Nuremburg to me... "we just do as we're told, Guv..." 
I don't think so.)

Anyway, that is only ONE definition, and it is just your usual boilerplate 
trotted out by rote, without due thought. It is too vague to be applied to 
this particular argument.

Even if it were true,  it still doesn't alter the fact that it was the 
obstinate resistance to change by people on the shop floor which caused the 
failure of OO COBOL. The effects of that failure are becoming more and more 
apparent and have consigned COBOL to a procedural backwater.

There is no coherent argument that can be made for the decline of COBOL 
being caused by middle managers. They simply don't know enough. They 
implement senior management policy which normally follows "fashion" or what 
the opposition are doing, or what the vendor salesperson advises, UNLESS 
there is a united groundswell of opinion from within the technical people. 
If everyone in COBOL shops had been clamouring for the new technology, 
middle managers would never have been able to stop it.The fact that most 
people resisted it fiercely means you can't blame the management. They 
actually went along with what the shop floor wanted.

> are you now saying that
> 'COBOL PROGRAMMERS' (caps original) are responsible for the resources
> accepted or rejected by their Managers?

Absolutely. Managers don't buy tools unless someone tells them to. Managers 
don't implement technical approaches (like OOP) unless someone suggests it 
could be a good (read, "money saving") idea... You know as well as I do that 
most middle managers simply are not technically qualified to make such 
decisions without advice from people who are so qualified. The programming 
team as a combined unit have a large effect in determining technical policy, 
standards, tools, and environments.

> 'Tis, indeed, The World Turned
> Upside-Down.

No it isn't. Management have ALWAYS had to be guided by technical 
people.They buy advice if their own people are not capable of providing it , 
or have no credibility.

If their own people treat them with contempt, then those people are unlikely 
to have much credibility. That's how you end up with "Airline Magazine" 
management policies.

People may seldom get the managers they want (although I've been pretty 
lucky in this regard... most of the people I've worked for have easily 
earned my respect...), but they will very often get the managers they 
deserve...

No-one has to tolerate bad management for any length of time. Either change 
it, or work somewhere else where they appreciate you more.

Of course there are bad managers. I acknowledged that in my original, (which 
you conveniently cut...)

What you do about your management may be the difference between enjoying 
going to work or actually not doing so.

All I'm saying here is that they were not  as responsibile for the decline 
of COBOL as COBOL programmers were.

Pete.
```

- **From:** "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>

- **References:** `<28aad$48a8708c$62148bf3$10578@ALLTEL.NET> <to4ja4d9275knaikl7oqrdckrd57e8bi21@4ax.com> <376ja4t2hj1etj0263s1l49e46chv3u16n@4ax.com> <6guo3sFhl7ohU1@mid.individual.net> <g8ega8$mq7$1@reader1.panix.com> <6h01t5FhnnttU1@mid.individual.net> <6h02qbFhqmmgU1@mid.individual.net> <vsnla45equ09t4s3vasm6duhgaoile9305@4ax.com>`

```

"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message
news:vsnla45equ09t4s3vasm6duhgaoile9305@4ax.com...

[snip]

> OO is no panacea.   It's just a tool.

Not quite!

As a noun, OO stands for Object-Orientation; as an
adjective, it stands for Object-Oriented.

OO is the result of a process commonly called the OO
paradigm. The OO paradigm consists of tools and
techniques. OO analysis, OO design, and OO programming
are techniques. OO programming languages are tools.

-----

One problem I have is that no matter how many times
OO, the adjective, is used, OO, the result, may not
happen. In those cases, the result is procedural. Thus,
it often seems to me, claims of success with OO are,
in reality, claims for the superiority of OO technology in
achieving procedure-orientation.

Another problem is that some seem to consider OO and
procedural to be polar opposites. In fact, all imperative
programming langauges are inherently procedural; thus
an imperative OO programming language is a procedural
programming language.

If procedure-orientation may be achieved compentently
with a procedure-oriented programming language, then
what is the value of OO in this context?

If the only value of OO, in this context, is the alleged
superiority of OO technology, then would it not make
more sense to create better procedure-oriented
technology?
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ The Limits of OO (was Re: How competent are we)

_(thread depth: 10)_

- **From:** "klshafer@att.net" <klshafer@att.net>
- **Date:** 2008-08-19T14:07:48-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ce460955-e65d-491b-8bfe-894b15d935a4@25g2000hsx.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<28aad$48a8708c$62148bf3$10578@ALLTEL.NET> <to4ja4d9275knaikl7oqrdckrd57e8bi21@4ax.com> <376ja4t2hj1etj0263s1l49e46chv3u16n@4ax.com> <6guo3sFhl7ohU1@mid.individual.net> <g8ega8$mq7$1@reader1.panix.com> <6h01t5FhnnttU1@mid.individual.net> <6h02qbFhqmmgU1@mid.individual.net> <vsnla45equ09t4s3vasm6duhgaoile9305@4ax.com> <_u-dnTKGZZyftTbVnZ2dnUVZ_sDinZ2d@posted.mid-floridainternet>`

```
On Aug 19, 4:08 pm, "Rick Smith" <ricksm...@mfi.net> wrote:
> "Howard Brazee" <how...@brazee.net> wrote in message
>
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> achieving procedure-orientation.

If I understand you correctly, Mr. Rick Smith, then I have read of
similar observations from within the OO crowd itself, when to their
dismay, the end result has more presence of "service objects" than the
OO purists would like, rather than "true objects", which are
preferred.

>
> Another problem is that some seem to consider OO and
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>

If all the universities are teaching are Java and C##, and neglecting
the purely procedural languages, then the "value" is that the OO
language is the only one "known" to the practitioner?

>
> If the only value of OO, in this context, is the alleged
> superiority of OO technology, then would it not make
> more sense to create better procedure-oriented
> technology?

Robert Glass has been stating as much for some time.

Which brings me to the reason that I changed the Subject line, and
citations which are more important than my own subjective ramblings
above.

Please consider the following as representative of giving a "voice" to
many who may have intuited certain misgivings about OO, regardless of
how well articulated or whether expressed emotionally or factually:

1. That misgivings have existed about OO has been known for some time.
Consider, for example, this excerpt:

_Software Lives Too Long_, in the July/Aug 1998 issue of _IEEE
Software_, by John C. Munson.

"There is one caveat to the destruction and replacement
of old programs: they will likely be rewritten in an object-oriented
language such as C++, which will make the new versions of the programs
almost completely unmaintainable. Global variables have always been
major maintenance headaches—and the new OO design methodologies now
make functions global."

2. A very exhaustive 2002 study undertaken by Dr. Paul Jorgensen,
Professor of Computer Science at Grand Valley State University, Grand
Rapid, MI, and author of the respected _Software Testing: A
Craftsman's Approach_,

__Has the Object Oriented Paradigm Kept its Promise?__

which can be found at -
http://www.informatikdidaktik.de/HyFISCH/Informieren/Programmiersprachen/OOPromisesAndReality.pdf

Short Answer: Not very well.

3. And now finally, what Robert Glass, indirectly, may have to say
about this. For those unfamiliar with him, you can read a bit here:
http://www.developerdotstar.com/mag/bios/software_practitioner.html

And what he is currently doing:

"The Software Practitioner is a bimonthly newsletter written by and
for people who build software for a living. It is not written by
journalists (who typically know too little about software) or
theorists (who know too little about practice)."

From a recent issue (May/June 2008) of _Software Practitioner_

"Is 'Progress' in Software Engineering All Gain?"
by Abhinav Shahi and Paul Wernick, University of Hertfordshire,
England

Copyright keeps me from posting the article in its entirety. Those
interested may contact me directly by Reply to Author and I'll seek
permission to send you a sample issue .pdf. In the meantime, here are
some "fair use" excerpts:

<Excerpts from Summary and Conclusions>:

"All of the literature cited above either explicitly or implicitly
brings into question the claim that thinking in an OO way is in some
way 'natural'. Were it so, the newcomers would find it easier to use
OO than non-OO approaches, whereas the evidence seems to point in
exactly the opposite direction... It seems overall that a Procedural
oriented method is a better candidate to be called 'natural' than OO."

"...instead of being a change for the better, in fact the move to OO
has resulted in software engineers talking in terms which are less
easily accessible to non-experts."

"To our surprise, despite the support from major figures in software
engineering, the claim to greater naturalness is refuted by
experimental work. In fact, in this regard it emerges that previous
approaches were better than current ones, and far from gaining
anything in intelligibility of our designs by non-experts by moving to
OO programming there has been a deterioration."

"Overall, the move to OO has resulted in non-specialists, the people
who are actually buying, paying for, and using a system, being unable
to participate fully in the negotiations on key areas of system
development such as the need to take into account in designing a
system structure the flexibility necessary to support potential change
to that system in the future. Essentially, by adopting a less
'natural' approach important stakeholders have been frozen out of
areas of decision-making to which they are well able to contribute."

<End of Excerpts>

My take on all of this:

Can the reluctance of the CoBOL community to quickly and universally
adapt the OO paradigm be re-interpreted in view of the above?

If "resistance to change" is a valid explanation, is "a desire to
preserve a more 'natural' approach (i.e. procedural)" an equally valid
explanation?

Ken
```

```
On Tue, 19 Aug 2008 14:07:48 -0700 (PDT), "klshafer@att.net"
<klshafer@att.net> wrote:

>"There is one caveat to the destruction and replacement
>of old programs: they will likely be rewritten in an object-oriented
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>make functions global."

That's if they are implemented as the theory says they should be
implemented.    But in real life, I have only seen compromises at a
system level.

That said, another compromise is a change in the level of what we are
willing to test.    This has happened before - when our compiler is
upgraded, we trust that the commands still work properly until we have
evidence to the contrary.   With global objects, we don't test
globally, but trust that local tests are sufficient until we have
evidence to the contrary.
```

```
"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
news:6h1dtfFi1v5eU1@mid.individual.net...
>
> "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote in message
> news:_u-dnTKGZZyftTbVnZ2dnUVZ_sDinZ2d@posted.mid-floridainternet...

[snip]

> > If procedure-orientation may be achieved compentently
> > with a procedure-oriented programming language, then
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> today who DO know about them...)

Pete, it looks like you may have copied a brochure for an
object-oriented programming language. The context was
procedure-orientation and 'reflection, dynamic programming,
functiional programming' seem entirely outside that context.
Also, procedure-orientation uses data that is exposed; not
encapsulated.

Re-use is accomplished through inheritance and an improved
procedure-oriented programming language could increase
re-use by implementing procedural inheritance. The value of
polymorphism in procedure-orientation is questionable.

This leaves 'lowered maintenance, removal of regression
testing'. I agree that extending a proper OO system has
these qualities; but maintenance to effect a change in design
can be horrific. [By 'proper OO system', I am referring to
full object-orientation, such as a stand-alone application or
embedded system; not the use of OO, the adjective, to
achieve procedure-orientation.]


> I'm not going to waste time debating OO here (I already wasted years doing
> that in this forum and got a heap of abuse for my trouble).

I agree that any intense debates about OO are no longer
worthwhile; but, occasionally, a new thought or direction
might prove interesting.

-----

With Tropical Storm Fay meandering through Florida,
I will probably be spending a bit more time trying to
figure out where it's headed next. The center-line of the
current projected path crosses about 30 miles north of
my location and on the weak side of the storm; but the
'cone of uncertainty', at this time, includes 50 miles south
and the strong side of the storm.

I can think about OO anytime; but I can be fascinated
by storms only when they occur and T.S. Fay is a
strange one.
```

```
On Wed, 20 Aug 2008 16:23:17 -0400, "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
wrote:

>I can see the case where 'computer programming language'
>is a paradigm, while a specific computer programming
>language is a tool; but I just can't seem to imagine that a
>paradigm is a tool.

Anything used to create or facilitate the creation of an end result is
a tool.

How do we accomplish an end result?   We use whatever tools work best
- some of those tools are physical, others are methodologies.   Some
tools work better (however "better" is defined) than others at
producing a given end result.

Of course, we don't all have the same final goals.   My "end result"
program is just a tool for the salesman in achieving his goals.   The
salesman's "end result" is just a tool for the CEO in achieving his
goals.  The CEO's strategy is just a tool for the stockholder in
achieving his goals.
```

```
First, data and automobiles are not paradigms, generally.
Second, I don't always need 'only' to understand its
presence--insert 'only' before 'tools'. Third, the only
valid answers are 'such an occupation exists' and 'no such
occupation exists'.

> >If I were a merchant, could I buy and sell paradigms?
>
> That kind of a merchant is generally called a teacher.
-----
merchant
1.One whose occupation is the wholesale purchase and
   retail sale of goods for profit.
2.One who runs a retail business; a shopkeeper.
-----
A teacher is not a merchant, though I had an Algebra
teacher I called Miss Merchant.

> >The so-called Procedural Paradigm consists of Stuctured
> >Analysis, Structured Design, and Structured Progamming.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> hammer without using all of those components?

If 'hammer' is a paradigm and without knowing its
components, could one even learn it?

> >By these questions, I am led to conclude that paradigms
> >are not real; but tools must be.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> But I'm not seeing your path.

No paradigm is a tool.

This definition seems closest the discussion of OO.
-----
tool
3.Something regarded as necessary to the carrying out
of one's occupation or profession
-----
If one's occupation or profession is OO Software Developer,
knowledge of the OO paradigm and its components seems
necessary to be such a developer. However, "the carrying
out of" suggests, to me, such things as computer programs
to plan, document (both text and diagrams), edit source code,
compile, and test.
```

```
can't be a tool because of X".   So I gave examples of tools that had
X, and you said - but they aren't paradigms.

It's not important what these things that work exactly like tools do

treat them as end-products, we can call them whatever we want.
```

```
In a sense, isn't a paradigm the tool one uses to mentally organize the 
world -- the theory of how the world works?  It could be a tool in the 
sense that it defines the approach that a person takes toward problem 
solving.  Consider Einsteinian physics or Newtonian physics vs. Quantum 
mechanics.  Each works and one can select which approach is a better fit 
for the problem at hand.  Perhaps one could consider a paradigm to be a 
meta-tool, defining the use of tools?
```

```

"Richard Brady" <rrllbrrady@worrlldnet.att.net> wrote in message
news:zrBrk.175049$102.75060@bgtnsc05-news.ops.worldnet.att.net...
> Rick Smith wrote:

[snip]

> > No paradigm is a tool.
> >
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
> meta-tool, defining the use of tools?

Some time back, I described the clerical paradigm that, it
seems to me, occurs often in COBOL applications. Basically,
the pattern is to do whatever procedures a clerk would do.
This is a theory about how one aspect of the world works.

The name of the paradigm suggests the pattern, the pattern
suggests the practice, the practice is carried out using tools.
If one were to consider the practice to be a tool, then the
paradigm could be likened to a toolbox. But, this raises the
question of whether a toolbox is a tool.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Re How competent are we

_(thread depth: 7)_

- **From:** Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2008-08-19T08:25:52-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<p9lla492h32fhnpticin3c4n4ufe8o4056@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<28aad$48a8708c$62148bf3$10578@ALLTEL.NET> <to4ja4d9275knaikl7oqrdckrd57e8bi21@4ax.com> <376ja4t2hj1etj0263s1l49e46chv3u16n@4ax.com> <6guo3sFhl7ohU1@mid.individual.net> <g8ega8$mq7$1@reader1.panix.com> <6h01t5FhnnttU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Wed, 20 Aug 2008 01:59:00 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>There is no coherent argument that can be made for the decline of COBOL 
>being caused by middle managers. They simply don't know enough.

That's not sufficient reason.   Are you saying that people who do know
enough are responsible for its decline?

There are lots of contributors, but IMHO, the main one is that the
industry has changed, and more tools are available.    Many of those
tools are much more cost-effective for the tasks they are used for.

There hasn't been a good economic reason to convert existing CoBOL to
OO CoBOL, and the advantages of OO CoBOL over some of the other modern
tools hasn't been shown to be worth the costs of compilers and
training.

When our shops add new tools for new functions, or when our shops
switch to new platforms, we get serious about learning those tools.
(Contractors need to anticipate their market needs, and sometimes
guess wrong).   Until then, we play with some of the tools that are
inexpensively available to us.    But who's going to go home and
program in OO CoBOL without seeing a payback?    We're much more
likely to use web tools and languages that will give us products we
will use.

Sure there are places where old CoBOL is still being used - but no
language will dominate business the way CoBOL used to.   We have far
more niches to fill, with different tools working best for different
needs.
```

```
From http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuremberg_Defense :

--begin quoted text:

The Nuremberg Defense is a legal defense that essentially states that the 
defendant was "only following orders" ("Befehl ist Befehl", literally 
"order is order") and is therefore not responsible for his crimes. The 
defense was most famously employed during the Nuremberg Trials, after 
which it is named.

--end quoted text

From http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuremberg_trials :

--begin quoted text:

The Nuremberg Trials were a series of trials most notable for the 
prosecution of prominent members of the political, military, and economic 
leadership of Nazi Germany.

--end quoted text

From http://www.catb.org/jargon/html/G/Godwins-Law.html :

--begin quoted text:

[Usenet] "As a Usenet discussion grows longer, the probability of a 
comparison involving Nazis or Hitler approaches one." There is a tradition 
in many groups that, once this occurs, that thread is over, and whoever 
mentioned the Nazis has automatically lost whatever argument was in 
progress.

--end quoted text

Conclusions will be left to the Gentle Readers.

DD
```

```
> Don't be sad.  People will be people.

As one who generally delights in the Human condition and enjoys diversity of 
culture I am not saddened by Human behaviour in general, accepting that 
there will be good and bad.

But I perceive this as being closer to home. I have great respect for the 
achievements and careers of most of the posters here. I understand their 
backgrounds and have shared it. I therefore find it saddening when they 
exhibit arrogance and resistance to change. It especially irritates me when 
I know that there are good young people coming into the industry who already 
know more than many of the posters here.(self included).

It is sad to see the mighty reduced to grumpy old men re-living former 
times. That, in and of itself would be harmless enough, but the contention 
is that they are the ONLY ones who got it right and the world, including the 
new wave of young programmers, simply doesn't get it.

That is the primary reason why I will be limiting my posts here.

Pete.
```

```
proving
> to be... but, it is hypothetical, we'll never know...Certainly the OO
> paradigm has been embraced by rest of the world and it is hard to imagine
> serious computing in today's world without it.
>

Pete - has it never occurred to you that your success with OO is not due to
overwhelming automatic virtues in OO but to the meticulous planning you do
before writing anything?  Are you prepared to look us in the eye and tell us
that a program written in the Bad Olde ways wouldn't be just as good as your
results if planned so well?  (Not including here matters of distribution and
change because that's another subject).

> ... Things are changing. I don't like change. There's no need for it to
> change; I can handle it fine the way it is. Now I'm all bewildered by the
> change. That's what you get when you let managers run things...
>

Think rather, that people don't mind change all that much - because it is
after all inescapable - but object most strongly to having change imposed on
them.  Especially when it's being pushed by people much junior to
themsleves!  Experience does have pointed, sad lessons to bring to bear.

> Sad.
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> brilliant people fresh out of community college who, having no experience
of
> the real world, have no idea how a "good" program should act and how it
> should be written. "
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> Maybe... I would agree that experience certainly helps and most of us try
to
> make each program we write better than the last one we wrote (I do that
> still today), but is it really fair to dismiss the youngsters coming out
of
> colleges today, having paid their dues, spent years of study and having
had
> to grapple with stuff that we never did, as if the only area they can be
> faulted in is their lack of experience?
>

Unless you are a very rare youngster who will sit back and study the problem
properly, instead of urgently bashing out code (as all of us did at first),
then you will write things that make little sense in light of experience.
You may ask the user to make a choice based on something that's obvious to
you, the programmer, but to the user is utterly opaque, for instance.

(Sort of along these lines: what's the rationale behind the Windows standard
menu (or cascading windows choices) behaviour of dumping the whole stack and
requiring the use to go back to the very first choice, every time?  I've
never understood that).

I'm not dismissing them   I'm just saying that their opening efforts will
not compare favourably to their later ones.   And it's our misfortune that
so many popular products were evidently produced that way.  Not altogether
their fault, of course, since marketing required that the stuff be got out
of the door.  A friend of mine has a saying: " you can't get away from
f...d-up thinking": meaning that if you make a bad design choice at first
that it's exceedingly difficult to repair that choice.



>
> What the Hell is this exclusive club of "oldtimers" who were the only
people
> who ever wrote "good" software, who did it using good solid procedural
COBOL
> constructs (the ONLY way to program computers) , did it in spite of
> horrendous know-nothing managers, and had the rug pulled out from under
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> Pete.

Jeez, you're sounding districtly peevish today.  And you've read a hell of a
lot more into the statements than is actually there.

PL
```

#### ↳ Re: COBOL utilities for businesses

- **From:** epc8@juno.com
- **Date:** 2008-08-20T16:59:34-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<2baa6efc-0b98-4dff-9a84-e7e30087d27d@m3g2000hsc.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<78ee9dc5-2bf4-44ae-b797-0f70af4b5605@d1g2000hsg.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Aug 18, 4:42 am, x01001x <xem...@softhome.net> wrote:
> What are some real world examples of software utilities written in
> COBOL that would have been used from 1980 until 1999?
> What kind of companies would be using these utilities?

About 20 years ago, I was asked to evaluate just such a program. It
ran on a PC-XT class computer. The setup and documentation was poor.
After invoking its batch file, it was obvious that it relied on
ANSI.SYS (ANSI character control sequences) for cursor positioning,
etc as the screen was filled with garbage including numerous left
brackets. After adding ANSI.SYS to CONFIG.SYS and rebooting, I was
treated to an ornate display for a simple application.

The system first pre-loaded an external file handler, FABS (Fast
Access B-Tree System) as a TSR (terminate and stay resident) program.
I presume this was for indexed files. Next a COBOL runtime loaded the
main program. Sorry I can't remember EXACTLY which COBOL.

The amusing part to me was this was an approximately $500 program that
acted as a simple tool inventory database for a construction business.
It had various menus which allowed building a database of tools in
each tool shed and then checking tools in and out, etc. I had to
chuckle as this was being marketed to small scale contractors with
small shops. I found it hard to believe that in such a shop, people
would actually take the time to check tools in or out. I also wondered
if an ordinary PC would survive the rigors of the shop environment -
dirt, sawdust, oil and grease included.

The final laugh came when I tried to search for a particular tool. The
screen prompts asked me to "Make your selection of the tool search
optimization algorithm." Needless to say I did not recomment that
particular program....

Of course, at that time, similarly crappy small business software was
also being written, more likely in some form of Microsoft Basic!

- e
```

```
On Thu, 21 Aug 2008 07:53:47 -0500, "Michael Mattias" <mmattias@talsystems.com> wrote:

><epc8@juno.com> wrote in message 
>news:2baa6efc-0b98-4dff-9a84-e7e30087d27d@m3g2000hsc.googlegroups.com...
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>Crap is source-language agnostic.

Cobol shops usually have formal institutionalized crap; other shops usually do not.

 Many or most Cobol shops have a 1980s standard containing a long list of Thou Shall Nots,
which are rigorously enforced. Shops using other languages either have no standard or a
positive, suggestive one.
```

## Thread: Error 98,90 — 9 msgs

### Error 98,90

- **From:** Gio <giovanni_dimaio@virgilio.it>
- **Date:** 2008-08-19T03:02:14-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<c4b861d9-54cc-4099-8c4c-0e34256b381a@m73g2000hsh.googlegroups.com>`

```
Hi Everybody,
I'm using Acucobol 4.3 and experiencing a strange corruption error
98,90.
All the workstations are running WindowsXPpro and the Server is
running 2003Server.
Did anyone ever face this problem before ?
Thanks for your help.

Giovanni
```

#### ↳ Re: Error 98,90

- **From:** sgbojo@gmail.com
- **Date:** 2008-08-19T07:23:32-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1161da6e-9592-49d8-ae77-87c769747062@r35g2000prm.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<c4b861d9-54cc-4099-8c4c-0e34256b381a@m73g2000hsh.googlegroups.com>`

```
98,90 is pretty well known as a file locking issue when using mapped
drives.

The main reason for file error 98,90's is when runtimes terminate
incorrectly.  Normally when the runtime shuts down it will update some
information in the Vision file header and subtract itself from the
user count that is also in the Vision File header.  If the information
in the header is incorrect the runtime will report a corruption
error.

An invalid user count in the Vision File header can cause the error
98,90 to be returned as well.  You can check Vision files to determine
if they have a non-zero user count by using the vutil -check -a
{filename} command.  Non-zero user counts when no one is accessing the
file indicate improper shutdown.

•Recommendations.

You could try setting the runtime configuration variable FLUSH_ON_OPEN
to "1" as this will cause the runtime to flush the system buffers on
the first WRITE, REWRITE or DELETE on an indexed file opened for I/O.
The purpose of this is to keep the user count in the header
accurate.

You might also wish to Set V_BUFFERS 0 and V_LOCK_METHOD 0 in the
runtime configuration files.  The default setting for V_LOCK_METHOD of
"0" (zero) causes Vision to lock the first byte of the file for every
access to the file (both reads and updates). This ensures that the
process is not interfered with by another process.

V_BUFFERS sets the number of indexed block buffers to allocate. These
buffers are used to improve the performance of indexed files. Each
buffer is 512 bytes plus some overhead. Increasing the number of
buffers can improve file performance. Decreasing the number conserves
memory. The value of V_BUFFERS has no effect on versions of ACUCOBOL-
GT that do not use Vision files. The value of V_BUFFERS can range from
zero (no buffering) to 2097152. The default value is 64.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Error 98,90

- **From:** Gio <giovanni_dimaio@virgilio.it>
- **Date:** 2008-08-20T01:01:35-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3ea321ec-e6b0-4fe1-a172-764bd9857cec@r66g2000hsg.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<c4b861d9-54cc-4099-8c4c-0e34256b381a@m73g2000hsh.googlegroups.com> <1161da6e-9592-49d8-ae77-87c769747062@r35g2000prm.googlegroups.com>`


###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Error 98,90

- **From:** sgbojo@gmail.com
- **Date:** 2008-08-20T06:43:14-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<dee8669d-ccc3-4b3a-8ddb-9ef9046f761b@n38g2000prl.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<c4b861d9-54cc-4099-8c4c-0e34256b381a@m73g2000hsh.googlegroups.com> <1161da6e-9592-49d8-ae77-87c769747062@r35g2000prm.googlegroups.com> <3ea321ec-e6b0-4fe1-a172-764bd9857cec@r66g2000hsg.googlegroups.com>`

```
It is a system issue due to that you are using Windows mapped drives
to have a program execute on PC-A while the data files reside on a
mapped drive to server b.

A Microsoft Windows feature called opportunistic locking (or
“oplocks”) for shared files is available for multi-user network
environments to improve system performance.  Opportunistic locking is
a file access method that allows the caching of data files on a user’s
workstation while that user reads or updates the files.  This feature
is intended to optimize network performance by reducing the number of
read and write operations that travel across the network when data
files are modified.
Opportunistic locking may cause problems in peer-to-peer networks that
accommodate a large number of users and heavy database activity
without a dedicated server.  We have observed that opportunistic
locking may result in file corruption of shared files.  When a second
user opens a particular file, the system retrieves the file from the
first user’s local machine and allows file access to the second user.
The possibility for corruption exists in the “handoff” of the file
among the users who share it.
Recognizing that file integrity is an important issue for any company,
Microsoft has recommended disabling this feature in any network that
uses its Access database.  (Refer to Microsoft Knowledge Base Article
300216 at support.microsoft.com for more details.)  Similarly, Novell
currently distributes its systems with this feature disabled by
default.  Acucorp also recommends disabling this feature to ensure
file integrity. Disabling opportunistic locking may degrade
application performance significantly, particularly if the application
reads and writes data across a network at a rate of only a few bytes
at a time.  In contrast, Acucorp’s runtime reads and writes indexed
files in blocks of 512 or 1024 bytes each, depending on the block size
set at file creation.  It uses standard C file access routines—open(),
read(), write(), seek(), and close()—to accomplish file-handling
operations.  This behavior allows several bytes at a time to be
transmitted across a network. Users should note that file OPENs are
relatively expensive operations, especially on Windows systems.
Eliminating non-essential file OPENs could make an application more
efficient and in turn improve application performance.  Users might
also consider the acquisition of a dedicated server for networks that
facilitate database access.
```

```
Hello Gio,

this program does not really solve the problem, but it prevents negative
impact. The user count of Vision files counts up one when a process opens
the file and count down one if the file is closed or then runtime shuts
down in a regular way. But if the runtime does not shut down regular (for
example if the client is killed) this counter grows. Since it is a 16 bit
number it will grow to 32767, then jump to -32768 and some day it will be 0
even though someone accesses the file. In this situation other processes
trying to open the file will get the error code 98,89 or 98,90.

The regular way would be a "vutil -rebuild" with the vutil tool from
AcuCorp. But this means you have to shut down the application and with
large files this may take several hours.

With my vuserset tool you may call i.e.

vuserset filename 100

to set the user count of a file to 100. Before you use this program you
shuld verify with "vutil -check filename" that it is really the user count
wich causes your problem.

If you have a Vision V.4 or V.5 file you must not use this tool on the
extension files (*.v??, *.d??). Run the program only on the main file and
make sure you have a backup. The number must not be lower than the actual
number of opens on the file and it should not be unnecessary high. When I
use the program I count the number of running processes, multiply it with
the number of opens on this file in a process and add about 20%.

If this solves your problem you may use this tool also prophylactical.

Lothar
```

```
OK, folks, I've learned of this...

There's an opportunity in Indianapolis, IN, with several slots still
open, or so I'm told. The particulars are:

o IBM mainframe (specific OS version unknown, but presumed to be Z/OS)
o COBOL
o IDMS (other Codasyl DB work maybe will fit)
o ADSO
o JCL
o government/defense/financial application
o maintenance and support of system
o must be U.S. citizen
o overtime may be available
o rate in the neighborhood of $30/hr W-2, $36-$37/hr 1099 or corp-to-
corp
o minimum of one year, but almost certain to be three years, and has
potential to go much longer (some contractors have been there up to 9
years already)

I understand rate is moderate/low, but project is long term, and
Indianapolis is a moderate to low cost-of-living city. It might work
for some of you.

I know two, maybe three brokers, that I have experience with who are
working the client. I'll brief you on them, and you can make your own
inquiries and take your pick on who to represent you.

Those of you with "less than perfect fit" credentials still encouraged
to contact me. I think they'll still want to submit you.

Contact me via Reply to Author.

Thanks,
Ken
```

```
It is my understanding that Princeton is _the very best_ of the Ivy
League. That is worth a lot.

On the other hand, it was only the other day, as I was driving back
home after a funeral visitation for a co-worker's wife, and taking a
route that I would not otherwise take, that I found myself on Indiana
State Road 9, and saw displayed in all its prominence, a highway sign
proudly proclaiming "Highway of the Vice Presidents".

Route 9 probably goes near Huntington (birthplace of Dan Quayle and
maybe the site of the Dan Quayle Museum, I kid you not), and maybe
also through the birthplaces of the other four Hoosiers who have been
Vice-President. See http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_United_States_Vice_Presidents_by_place_of_primary_affiliation.

And as I type this, Sen. Evan Bayh is still in contention to be our
sixth VP. Go, Evan!

Notice that Indiana has had more Vice Presidents than any other state
save New York. So we are even Number 2 at being Number 2.

But, ahhhhh, that is the "sweet spot" for Hoosiers. We're not even
_trying_ to be Number 1. Number 2 is just right. It's the high blade
of grass that get cut after all, so les' jus' stay out-of-the-
limelight just a bit.

So instead of sending your kids to Princeton, try just down the road
from Indianapolis, at Indiana University, or Ball State. Just like
David Letterman. After all, he never has been as popular as Jay Leno,
just funnier.

It'll be cheaper than Princeton. And more fun.

Ken
```

```
What is best? [in life is to crush your enemies and see them driven before

I once visited a client in Harvard Square. During a break, he suggested I 
take a stroll.

When I returned, he asked how I liked the neighborhood. I said: "I'm willing 
to admit you have more PhDs per square foot than I'm used to, more books in 
your libraries, and larger endowments. But on the two things that really 
matter, Texas has you beat."

"What's that?" he asked.

"Pretty women and football teams."

"I'd believe that," he admitted, "...and I've never been to Texas."

"Son," I opined, "you got women wandering around here that, if they were in 
Texas, there'd be a bounty on 'em."

He hung his head and muttered: "Did you notice their socks don't match?..."
```

```
On Aug 22, 9:58 am, "HeyBub" <hey...@NOSPAMgmail.com> wrote:
> klsha...@att.net wrote:
>
[snip]
> When I returned, he asked how I liked the neighborhood. I said: "I'm willing
> to admit you have more PhDs per square foot than I'm used to, more books in
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Well, HeyBub, where's I'm from it's Pretty Women and _Basketball_
teams.

Which reminds me of a story which illustrates what we are talking
about...

Forty years ago I found myself attending Rose Polytechnic (now Rose
Hulman Institute of Technology, or RHIT) in the backwaters of Terre
Haute, IN. Was then a very, very good, even _excellent_, undergraduate
science and engineering school, and still is, right up there just a
tad below MIT. (Remember here we are talking undergraduate program
only.) Really, all in all, a somewhat surprising institution to find
in our modest state of Indiana.

It was the first quarter of my college experience. I studied hard, was
doing well (in fact, I finished the quarter _very_ well), and my
fellow students were certainly plenty smart, interesting, and the
appropriate measure of rowdy for a rather nerdy bunch. I had
significant scholarship assistance, so the money issue was OK. And
yet...

Thing is, back then it was exclusively male. (They later dropped that,
it is now coed, but still only nominally so, with probably 80-90% of
the students still male.) And it was small (only about 800-1000
students total), and the only majors were the sciences, math, and
engineering majors.

I can't remember the exact date, it must have been some 1967 mid-
autumn afternoon, but I can remember the experience vividly. I was
taking a walk around the rather pretty campus, after lunch, and I had
a few saltine crackers, and I crunched them up, threw them into one of
the two ponds which graced the campus, and watched the fish come up
and gobble them. "This is _fun_!" I thought to myself.

Then I took a step back, mentally, and probably physically, and
realized with a small inner voice "You are at a place where your idea
of fun is to feed crackers to the fishes."

I was in an excellent place, but it was a very, very _narrow_ place.

The very next semester I was enrolled at Indiana University -
Bloomington (IU). Just in time for IU to play (and lose) in the Rose
Bowl (they have not returned since, and they offer us little hope of
returning again in my lifetime.) But that is not the end of the story.
For this is, after all, Indiana, and not Texas.

By 1972 I had met there the woman who I was to marry. About the same
time Robert Montgomery Knight had arrived. And by 1975, by the time I
got my Masters degree, IU had won the NCAA. There was much dancing in
the street that night, and back then, not as much mischief as we find
today at championship schools on win night.

Transferring from an _excellent_ school to a _good_ school with
basketball and women was, in my mind to this day, one of the best
decisions I have ever made. :-)

And to return to the nominal CoBOL thread, lest we have to change the
subject line prematurely, I believe, by analogy, that same kind of
attitude has served me well in my career. Rate/salary is a component,
but by no means the only component, in career satisfaction.

yours,
ken
```

```
I read a story ("The Mind Body Problem") wherein the heroine was married to 
a math professor. One of her observations was the following: In those 
disciplines where truth was empirically provable (math, physical sciences, 
engineering), neither the faculty nor students paid much mind to how they 
were dressed. In those areas where "truth" was a matter of majority vote - 
English, History, Philosophy, etc. - everybody wore uniforms: The men wore 
tweed jackets with leather elbow patches, the ladies wore basic black with 
pearls. She said that of ten students who wore a tennis shoe on one foot and 
a sandal on the other, eight would be math majors and the other two 
mathematical physicists.

From my original post, then, I conclude all the women I saw in Harvard 
Square were astronomy majors at Radcliffe.
```

```
On Aug 22, 11:11 am, SkippyPB <swieg...@Nospam.neo.rr.com> wrote:
> On Thu, 21 Aug 2008 11:42:36 -0700 (PDT), "klsha...@att.net"
>
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> I've lived in both).

Probably a "data blip" :-). Princeton will be back on top next year...
but yes, just coincidentally, the U.S. News and World Report
(USNWR)annual rankings are coming out Sept 1, and there are already
the news reports of it, with Harvard on top.

[snip]
>
> The two best schools in Indiana:  Purdue University, Notre Dame.

I'll check USNWR, but I would bet in their "all areas" ranking, that
is exactly right. The Notre Dame law program is rather superb,
significantly better than IU's.

As you probably recall from your tenure in the Hoosier State, the
legislature, in its wisdom I think of more than a century ago, made
sure that one university would never be too powerful, so they did a
"split" whereby Indiana University would get Law and Liberal Arts, and
Purdue would get the Engineering, and they would "share" some of the
stuff, but not too much, like the Sciences. Probably not
coincidentally, the capital (Indianapolis) was not to be the seat of
either (for the uninitiated, Purdue is in West Lafayette, and Indiana
Univ is in Bloomington.) It would be only relatively recently (the
last 10 years or so) that a joint effort of IU and Purdue, called
aptly enough, IUPUI (for IU-Purdue at Indianapolis), and pronounce,
ooey-pooey, would rise in stature to nearly that of IU or Purdue.

The USNWR news reports show that IU is no longer exclusively at the
bottom of the Big Ten (a spot now shared with Michigan State), which
is bad thing :-(, but allowing how the Big Ten (all public schools
except Northwestern) rank rather highly nationally, that is a _good_
thing :-).

It is usually worthwhile to drill drown to the school or department
level in your area of interest, though, in making your comparisons.
Though merely "good, or competent, or adequate" in many or most areas,
IU is top-drawer in its School of Music and also, I think, in certain
foreign languages (East Asian and Slavic, I believe, for example.)

But the best school for sciences and engineering, as I posted
elsewhere, is Rose Hulman Institute of Technology in Terre Haute, a
private school, with a large endowment from Tony Hulman, formerly of
the Indianapolis 500 Motor Speedway.

Large corporations in the state are happy to hire engineers from both
Purdue and Rose, but probably have a slight tilt toward Rose
graduates. Thing is, there just aren't that many of them.

None of these schools teach CoBOL any more, I am pretty sure. That
task has now been relegated to Ivy Tech Community College, now one of
the largest community colleges in the nation. That's where, perhaps
not concidentally, I am adjunct faculty. Will that be my destiny post-
contracting? :-)

ken
```

## Thread: SQL Expressions in Access, using Fujitsu COBOL — 10 msgs

_Topics: [`Compilers and vendors`](topics.md#compilers) · [`Databases and SQL`](topics.md#databases)_

### SQL Expressions in Access, using Fujitsu COBOL

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-08-22T15:40:11+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6h6qotFjca0jU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
I have been working intensely with MS Access  and Fujitsu COBOL over the 
last few weeks. (Actually I haven't been able to work too intensely because 
I have been recovering from illness, but on the occasions when I have been 
able to sit down and do some work, it has been intense... :-))

Most of the time it is perfectly satisfactory, and I have actually been 
surprised at how much SQL Language MS Access actually supports.

For the purposes for which it is required on this occasion, it is fine, but 
I have noticed some limitations when using it from COBOL.

1. There are some expressions in ANSI SQL which are not implemented in 
Access. COALESCE is one such.  You can get the same effect using an Access 
function called "Nz" (and there are also a couple of less elegant ways of 
dealing with returned nulls, including the old bog standard, indicator 
variables (ugly, but effective...)), but Nz doesn't work when invoked 
through ODBC from COBOL (using Fujitsu, which has an external ODBC.info 
file...). If I run a query in Access 2003 which uses the Nz function, it 
works perfectly. If I connect via OLEDB in C# and issue SQL containing it, 
it works perfectly. Write an ESQL query in COBOL and it crashes, returning 
SQLSTATE 37000 (normally a syntax error) and a message saying that an 
unknown function was found. Remove the Nz function and it works... (but, of 
course it doesn't help me detect nulls.)

2.  Access has always suffered from a bad reputation, which, since Access 
2000, has been thoroughly undeserved. The versions from then on used the 
same engine as SQL Server and are very effective RDB repositories if you use 
them as such and don't start trying to mix in Access "programming". However, 
it is annoying that functions which work perfectly well with Access and 
other languages, simply don't, with Fujitsu COBOL.

I'm curious as to whether this is a Fujitsu limitation or whether other 
people using Access from other versions of COBOL, via ODBC, have the same 
problems?

Pete.
```

#### ↳ Re: SQL Expressions in Access, using Fujitsu COBOL

- **References:** `<6h6qotFjca0jU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Fri, 22 Aug 2008 15:40:11 +1200, "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
wrote:

>I have been working intensely with MS Access  and Fujitsu COBOL over the 
>last few weeks. (Actually I haven't been able to work too intensely because 
…[31 more quoted lines elided]…
>problems?

Nz is not a SQL function. Try 

IsNull(column_name, "")

If that does not work, try

IIF(Not IsNull(column_name), column_name, "")
```

```
these functions and wondered whether it is limited to Fujitsu...
```
